Â¶ And so as I begin this, it is important, dear reader, that you learn that in this chapter there is more peril and fear than in the first about cauteries, and therefore, greater caution is required. Â¶ And I admonish you that you do not undertake rashly. For many kinds of people come seeking cure. This pain is found in legs, in hot places where there are no herbs or trees, and in earth that has no moisture; and by chance it may be born elsewhere than in the leg, and this happens because of rot that comes from under the skin, as it does from within the body, serpents and worms, lodged between skin and flesh. 	I marveled greatly how she moved thus from limb to limb, for I had never seen this sickness in this manner. Nevertheless, I had seen many who had well-felt pain move from place to place, but I had not seen it like this in the woman; and I thought that it was because the woman had a dry body, and uncovered veins, and because of this the constrained windiness appeared; and it does not appear in the same way to those who are fleshy, and who have moist bodies and covered veins.

When the two parts of the upper nose are broken or one only, for they are two bones; and the lower one is cartilaginous, and does not break, unless it be forced to bend or twist, and then heals.

The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the "bones," of which one is small and that follows the shoulder; and the other is large and is below, towards the little bone; by chance breaks the large bone alone, or the small one, and by chance both together. When the small bone is broken, the restoration is light, and the healing; and when the large one is broken, so is the healing bad, and more painful than when they are broken together. If it is the small bone that is broken alone, then it is convenient to extend it gently, and put a splint on it. Â¶ And if it is the large bone, then it is necessary to tighten it more; and extend it over one splint, and hold the shoulder of the hand higher than the others, and the little toes lower than the other toes, and let the sick person be crossed on him-self, and one splint in front of him higher, that the sick person does not suffer any labor; then extend the ministers of my arm to the lower part of his hand, or to one linen, and another minister to the other above. And then put the ministers on it evenly, according to its best form. Â¶ And if in the fracture pieces, then put each one in its place, and if there is any detached that grasps the heart, and you cannot restore it in its place, then make a splint as we have said before. Â¶ And if with the fracture there is a wound, I have spoken of it in the singular chapter; so take care of it. Â¶ And if at the beginning of the restoration sores come out, then glue one cloth of cerote made of rose oil and white wax, that it be neither too hard nor too soft; and tie one cloth over it subtly, until the sores are appeased. Then remove the cerote, and put the plaster of the mill powder with abundance of egg on it, then put the splints, and let the splint of the fracture be more outward, and a little wider than the others; and know that it must have six splints or arms in more dispositions: whether the fracture is in one of the bones, or two; and tighten more above the fracture than elsewhere, and every day less far away, as I said at the beginning of the chapter; and let the cloths that are on the fracture be soft and moist, and not hard nor rough; and let the thread, with which it is tightened, be linen, neither too thick nor too thin, as we have said. And reward the limb and the ligament after some days. And if any bad accident comes to the limb, such as itching that comes to the limb, then it is necessary that you raise the limb in hot water until the itchiness is appeased; and leave the limb not to be too tight or too loose. And then re-tighten, and if the ligaments are softened and the bones detached, and something like this happens, then address it. Â¶ And if take care that the nurses do not come to the limb because it is too tight, then it is necessary that you soften it one piece, and leave it so for some days, until the nurses come in, and then tighten it again. Â¶ And if none of these things that we have said come to the sick person, then do not unbind him until 20 days, or until when he feels better, then put his hands on his neck, and let his necks be equal, and take care of movement and agitation, and sleep on his back; and know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 35 days, and perhaps in 20 days, according to the compositions and the complexions of the virtues.

de plaie et de dislocation ensamble	...of wound and dislocation together
Quant plaie vient avec la dislocation, et on la vuet restorer, maintes fois avient que li malaides muert: si ne soies mie presumptuous en la cure, se tu nâ€™es saiges en lâ€™art et tu ne lâ€™as useit longement, et se tu nâ€™es pitous et misericors et tardis, ne mie hardis; et use au comencement de choses apaisans aposteme chaut, et laisse le malaide en la main Deu, se tu nâ€™i vois esperance de santeit; et quant tu i vois esperance, si ramoinne maintenant le membre au leu, ainsois que li apostemes chaus i viegne; et quant li membres est a leu, si aministre choses apaisans aposteme chaut, et cure la plaie, si com il covient, dâ€™oignemens desiccatis; et se avec la dislocation a brisure, et os menus desevrez, si les oste, et fai ce que nos avons dit en maladies simples; et te garde dâ€™entrer en voie de paour, selonc ce que jâ€™ai dit, car ce te vaut mues selonc ton Creator, et por la saveteit de tâ€™arme et de ton cors. Amen	When a wound comes with dislocation, and one wishes to restore it, many times it happens that the sick die: so be not presumptuous in the cure, if you are not wise in the art and have not used it long, and if you are not pitying and merciful and tardy, nor too bold; and use at the beginning soothing things, a hot poultice, and leave the sick in God's hand, if you see no hope of health; and when you see hope, then gently bring back the limb to its place, so that the hot poultice may come upon it; and when the limb is in place, administer soothing things, a hot poultice, and cure the wound as it befits, with dried ointments; and if with the dislocation there is a fracture, and small bones are broken, set them, and do what we have said in simple diseases; and guard yourself from entering into the path of fear, according to what I have said, for this will cause you loss according to your Creator, and for the safety of your limb and body. Amen
	This is an interesting text!  It seems to be offering advice on how to treat a dislocation that's accompanied by a wound. The author emphasizes caution, humility ("be not presumptuous"), and reliance on God's will.
Explicit le romen de fisique	The text doesn't explicitly lay out a system of "fisique" as we might understand it today (like physics).
